    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10270936932583301438">Written by the Arab historian Ibn Khaldun in 1377 which records an early view of universal history. Some modern thinkers view it as the first work dealing with the social sciences of sociology, demography, and cultural history. The Muqaddimah also deals with Islamic theology, historiography, the philosophy of history, economics, political theory, and ecology. It has also b</span>
  <span id="freeText10270936932583301438" style="display:none">Written by the Arab historian Ibn Khaldun in 1377 which records an early view of universal history. Some modern thinkers view it as the first work dealing with the social sciences of sociology, demography, and cultural history. The Muqaddimah also deals with Islamic theology, historiography, the philosophy of history, economics, political theory, and ecology. It has also been described as an early representative of social Darwinism.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_319708161" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/319708161" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/319708161?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691120546" href="/book/show/229567.The_Muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=14th-century">14th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=read-in-translation">read-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=africa">africa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer319708161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12523397307521301654">The Muqaddimah, an introduction to a universal history up to the author's own day (the 14th century AD) is repetitive, clumsy in places, has some curious choices of material, is maddeningly inexplicit occasionally, and entirely incredible. It is a true landmark in the study of history.<br/><br/>I first heard about this book as a student. It was a lead in to a lecture hall joke, the work had apparently been reconstructed from students' lecture notes after Ibn Khaldun's death (pause for punchline after the </span>
  <span id="freeText12523397307521301654" style="display:none">The Muqaddimah, an introduction to a universal history up to the author's own day (the 14th century AD) is repetitive, clumsy in places, has some curious choices of material, is maddeningly inexplicit occasionally, and entirely incredible. It is a true landmark in the study of history.<br /><br />I first heard about this book as a student. It was a lead in to a lecture hall joke, the work had apparently been reconstructed from students' lecture notes after Ibn Khaldun's death (pause for punchline after the lecturer scans the faces of the students before them), and this explains the repetitiveness. If an idea is important, it's worth explaining it more than once and very slowly so that everybody can note it down. Some things remain the same apparently.<br /><br />It's entirely deserved landmark status come from its bold originality. Ibn Khaldun is interested in the social structures that drive patterns of events in history. Actually he falls short here, but he explains a certain type of pattern of events that was central to Ibn Khaldun's experience of history, the rise and fall of dynasties from desert or nomadic tribes to dominance of urban civilisations which in turn decline and fall.<br /><br />The idea of explaining historical events with reference to social and economic structures is familiar now but in his time was something new. A quick comparison to contemporary European historical writings shows nothing like the self-conscious interest in the structures of civilisations that form the basis of Ibn Khaldun's work: group identities, forms of agriculture, forms of economic activity (including business profits and discussions on taxation), or the developmental pattern of state administrations. Although Ibn Khaldun's work is thoroughly Islamic, Sunni and indulgent to Sufism, his interest is in the human and material causation of events. Geography and Human cultures are the direct driving forces here. <br /><br />For Ibn Khaldun the harsh conditions of the desert or of a nomadic lifestyle created a unifying sense of group identity. Their poverty and desire for the goods that urban civilisations produced would draw such groups into conflict with settled communities, which when the latter were in decline they would overcome. The richer resource base of a more urbanised civilisation would allow the group to expand, the increasing sophistication of the ruling family would lead it to become isolated from the rest of their original group over time, this would lead to the decay and weakness of the state making it vulnerable to the next incoming group of nomadic peoples.<br /><br />As an explanatory formula for the recent North-African history of Ibn Khaldun's day, or for that matter the rise of the original Islamic Caliphate, the barbarian invasions of the western Half of the Roman Empire, or the advent of the Mongols or Manchus in northern-China, it was brilliant and insightful. His notion of cyclical cycles of growth and decay remind me of Weber's theory of political leadership which posits the possibility of a cycle from charismatic leadership to bureaucracy to stagnation. But it is not a universal formula for historical change. Maddeningly at one moment he implies that Islamic civilisation is undergoing a relative decline while the European Christian and Chinese civilisations are in a phases of upward growth but the idea is not explored explicitly. Irritatingly he often repeats a kind of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80890.Buddenbrooks_The_Decline_of_a_Family" title="Buddenbrooks The Decline of a Family by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Buddenbrooks</a> rule that a ruling dynasty can only endure for a fixed number of generations before collapsing (although the number of generations varies). This is an organic vision of human social life. Societies and ways of life are born, grow, mature, and die as much as people. Reading Ibn Khaldun one needn't restrict this idea to political entities either, the same processes occur everywhere. <br /><br />Given Ibn Khaldun's family background and occasional spells of time working in Muslim Spain it would have been deeply satisfying (at least for me) if he had looked at the phenomena of the Reconquista in the light of his theories. Later in his career, on a diplomatic mission to Castile, he was even invited by Pedro the Cruel to work for him - an opportunity which he declined. Unfortunate as I find the loss of a discussion on the decline of Muslim Spain this did leave him free to meet Timur the Lame whose rise and rule drawing on the group feeling of the Chingisids, the descendants of Genghis Khan, falls neatly into the framework set out by Ibn Khaldun. <br /><br />Ibn Khaldun goes further, because the cyclical succession of dynasties that he analyses do not merely effect political history but also impact on social and economic history. Initially the incomers are a destructive influence on agriculture and the built environment, but as they become acculturated to urban civilisation they change. A civilisation in decline, with a declining tax base will wither in other areas of economic and intellectual life, while a growing, expanding civilisation will have an expanding tax base, a richer eco-system of economic activity and have a higher level of cultural attainment in the arts and sciences. This gives the analysis an interplay between the moral simplicity and military strength of the incomers against all that urbanised civilisation has to offer, but ultimately it is the features of urbanised life and its labour specialisation that sustain human life. <br /><br />This is a work that is rich, fertile, and irritating by turn. Inspiring in its creativity, and maddening that it didn't inspire a North-African medieval Annales School. I'm sure that the Muqaddimah has more to offer to someone much more familiar with the rise and fall of the early Islamic dynasties than I am, but it is none the less clear that this is a major work of historical theory.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />PS according to Ibn Khaldun Euclid was a carpenter. I think I would have found school maths easier if the questions had been posed as practical carpentry problems.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12523397307521301654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110379172?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corinne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corinne" href="/user/show/3973919-corinne">Corinne</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110379172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18032289578014617029">I read this book because my History of Islam professor recommended it in a class last fall. 8 months later I looked through my notes and saw that I wrote down that I wanted to read it, and so I did.<br/><br/>This book surprised me in that I actually enjoyed reading it. I figured it would have good information but be tedious and boring. Not the case, Ibn Khaldun (and perhaps with the help of the translator) has a curious nature and a light humor in his work. He really steps back and looks at the world arou</span>
  <span id="freeText18032289578014617029" style="display:none">I read this book because my History of Islam professor recommended it in a class last fall. 8 months later I looked through my notes and saw that I wrote down that I wanted to read it, and so I did.<br /><br />This book surprised me in that I actually enjoyed reading it. I figured it would have good information but be tedious and boring. Not the case, Ibn Khaldun (and perhaps with the help of the translator) has a curious nature and a light humor in his work. He really steps back and looks at the world around him. <br /><br />I recommend this book to anyone who has an interest in the history and culture of North Africa and the Middle East. That said, it shouldn't be your introduction to it. I had taken a History of Islam and an Anthropology of Morocco class which gave me familiarity to religious terms, historical events, and an idea of who different groups were. So be curious about the subject before trying this book. Also, know something about modern science because he says some interesting things that are downright incorrect. Like how skin color has to do with the air. Good try buddy :-)<br /><br />In the end I gave it four stars. It does what it advertises, and it does it well!</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1110489264" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1110489264" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25360918-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465755029p2/25360918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1110489264?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/25360918-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691166285" href="/book/show/23528925-the-muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25360918-blair?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25360918-blair?shelf=history-islamic">history-islamic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25360918-blair?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25360918-blair?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25360918-blair?shelf=best-reviews">best-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25360918-blair?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1110489264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9535950732720857530"><i>“The intellectual sciences are natural to man, in as much as he is a thinking being.”</i><br/><br/>Truly Ibn Khaldun is a thinking being, creating this astonishing summary of the wisdom and knowledge of his time. He surpassed previous attempts to explain history, claiming with justification, <i>“We, on the other hand, were inspired by God. He led us to a science whose truth we ruthlessly set forth.”</i><br/><br/>His ruthless enumeration of the many sources of historical distortion reminds me of E. H. Carr’s essay <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/101323.What_Is_History_' title='What Is History? by Edward Hallett Carr' rel='nofollow noopener'>What Is Hist</a></span>
  <span id="freeText9535950732720857530" style="display:none"><i>“The intellectual sciences are natural to man, in as much as he is a thinking being.”</i><br /><br />Truly Ibn Khaldun is a thinking being, creating this astonishing summary of the wisdom and knowledge of his time. He surpassed previous attempts to explain history, claiming with justification, <i>“We, on the other hand, were inspired by God. He led us to a science whose truth we ruthlessly set forth.”</i><br /><br />His ruthless enumeration of the many sources of historical distortion reminds me of E. H. Carr’s essay <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/101323.What_Is_History_" title="What Is History? by Edward Hallett Carr" rel="nofollow noopener">What Is History?</a>. Carr points out the tendency of historians to cast all of history in terms of their present environment. For Khaldun, his world was one of many unstable and chaotic states. This gave him the opportunity to observe a lot of history in a short time, leading to his insight that the success of a civilization leads to its decay.<br /><br />The value of a book written by a brilliant mind from a different time and culture is that it gives us a different perspective on issues that still exist today. One such issue is the conflict between rational and religions thinking. Much of the book employs what I would call scientific materialism. In his own words, used while debunking astrology, <i>“astrologers give us only the astrological reason. They ought also to give us the terrestrial reason.”</i> Ibn Khaldun is also a believing Muslim, and other parts of the book reflect that. The conflict between religion and <i>terrestrial</i> reason co-exists uneasily in this book.<br /><br />Each of his chapters begins with a concise summary and ends with a reminder that suggests God’s will is best revealed by terrestrial observation. I will follow the same format to give a feel for how the book reads.<br /><br /><i>“God gives guidance and success to that which is correct.”</i><br /><br /><b>Insight Ahead of his Time</b><br /><br /><i>“Excessive taxation reduces revenue. Severity to students does them harm.”</i><br /><br />Ibn Khaldun often writes in abstract terms to give the big picture, from which I can only try to interpolate what his society was actually like in detail. For example, he displays an impressively modern understanding of economics. This suggests that a market economy was flourishing to some extent during his time. He presciently warns the ruler to stay out of commercial activity. Again, it suggests a centrally controlled economy was not the norm. The following explains the diminishing returns of taxation:<br /><br /><i>“When tax assessments and imposts upon the subjects are low, the latter have the energy and desire to do things. Cultural enterprises grow and increase, because the low taxes bring satisfaction… Eventually, the taxes will weigh heavily upon the subjects and overburden them. The result is that the interest of the subjects in cultural enterprises disappears, since when they compare expenditures and taxes with their income and gain and see the little profit they make, they lose all hope. Therefore, many of them refrain from all cultural activity. Thus, the total revenue continues to decrease. Finally, civilization is destroyed, because the incentive for cultural activity is gone.”</i><br /><br />Turning to education, he displays a sensitivity that can be seen as well ahead of his time:<br /><br /><i>“Severe punishment in the course of instruction does harm to the student, especially to little children, because it belongs among (the things that make for a) bad habit. It makes them lazy and induces them to lie and be insincere. That is, their outward behavior differs from what they are thinking, because they are afraid that they will have to suffer tyrannical treatment (if they tell the truth). Thus, they are taught deceit and trickery. This becomes their custom and character.”</i><br /><br />The book includes details on many other subjects, including an unexpectedly through description of midwifery. It even gives a reasonable account of evolution, though without natural selection. The insight into the knowledge of his time is fascinating.<br /><br /><i>“May God inspire us to choose the right course for ourselves, and may He make us profit from our beneficial actions. There is no Lord except Him."</i><br /><br /><b>The Darker Side of Geography</b><br /><br /><i>“The northern quarter of the earth has more civilization than the southern quarter. The reason thereof.”</i><br /><br />The knowledge of geography at the time is impressive, as Ibn Khaldun surveys the known world from Norway to “the islands of Korea”. He explains how geography and climate are linked to the rise of civilization in a way that reminds me of Jared Diamond in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1842.Guns__Germs__and_Steel_The_Fates_of_Human_Societies" title="Guns, Germs, and Steel The Fates of Human Societies by Jared Diamond" rel="nofollow noopener">Guns, Germs, and Steel: The Fates of Human Societies</a>. The understanding of what lay to the south was a little more vague, as this uncomfortable passage suggests:<br /><br /><i>“To the south of this Nile, there is a Negro people called Lamlam. They are unbelievers. There, they constitute the ordinary mass of slaves. Beyond them to the south, there is no civilization in the proper sense. There are only humans who are closer to dumb animals than to rational beings. They live in thickets and caves and eat herbs and unprepared grain. They frequently eat each other. They cannot be considered human beings… The Negro nations are, as a rule, submissive to slavery, because (Negroes) have little (that is essentially) human and have attributes that are quite similar to those of dumb animals, as we have stated.”</i><br /><br />This window into the past reminds us that these attitudes are far older than the modern West or even Islam. Ibn Khaldun is a geographical determinist rather than a racist, as he says black skin and other characteristics are a result of the hot sun, just as white skin was a result of a lack of sun in the uncivilized regions to the north. But there is no escaping the implications.<br /><br />The condition of slavery itself is frequently mentioned but never examined. In his time it was understood as the way things have always been, economically necessary for an educated elite to flourish. But it is convenient to believe the slaves are predestined to serve their purpose.<br /><br /><i>“This is how God proceeds with His creatures.”</i><br /><br /><b>Frontier Culture and the Contradictions of Civilization</b><br /><br /><i>“Sedentary people are much concerned with all kinds of pleasures. They are accustomed to luxury and success in worldly occupations and to indulgence in worldly desires. Therefore, their souls are colored with all kinds of blameworthy and evil qualities.”</i><br /><br />This book is the product of a highly educated man distilling the insights of an advanced civilization. But he seems nostalgic for a simpler society as represented by the desert Arabs. They are brave, resilient, and loyal to their group, and above all, hungry. It reminds me of the American frontier. One sometimes gets the impression that civilization is a cause for regret.<br /><br /><i>“The frugal inhabitants of the desert and those of settled areas who have accustomed themselves to hunger and to abstinence from pleasures are found to be more religious and more ready for divine worship than people who live in luxury and abundance. Indeed, it can be observed that there are few religious people in towns and cities, in as much as people there are for the most part obdurate and careless, which is connected with the use of much meat, seasonings, and fine wheat. The existence of pious men and ascetics is, therefore, restricted to the desert, whose inhabitants eat frugally.”</i><br /><br />He tells us that the rule of law of a sedentary civilization reduces the fortitude of the population by taking away their initiative. The desert Arabs could fend for themselves. The frontier myth is still with us – self-reliant Americans carry guns to be responsible for their own security.<br /><br />Although he seems to love the desert tribes as individuals, he has a less positive view on their collective impact on the civilization he is so ambivalent about.<br /><br /><i>“Places that succumb to the Arabs are quickly ruined. The reason for this is that (the Arabs) are a savage nation, fully accustomed to savagery and the things that cause it. Savagery has become their character and nature. They enjoy it, because it means freedom from authority and no subservience to leadership. Such a natural disposition is the negation and antithesis of civilization.”<br /><br />“Compared with sedentary people, they are on a level with wild, untamable (animals) and dumb beasts of prey. Such people are the Arabs.”</i><br /><br />One should remember that the author is an Arab, and praises the accomplishments of Arabs in other parts of his book. If the desert Arabs are the equivalent of the American frontier, perhaps this is the consequence of when frontier mentality collides with the civilized world.<br /><br /><i>“God has power to do what He wishes.”</i><br /><br /><b>A Civilization Lasts Only Four Generations</b><br /><br /><i>“It should be known that the world of the elements and all it contains comes into being and decays.”</i><br /><br />The theme of the book is that any society requires “group feeling” to hold it together, and civilization is built with “royal authority”. The sedentary lifestyle and luxuries at first contribute to the strength of a civilization, but then lead to its downfall. I think his explanation of why an empire based on a family dynasty last only four generations is worth recounting in detail:<br /><br /><i>“The four generations can be explained as the builder, the one who has personal contact with the builder, the one who relies on tradition, and the destroyer.”<br /><br />“The builder of the glory (of the family) knows what it cost him to do the work, and he keeps the qualities that created his glory and made it last. The son who comes after him had personal contact with his father and thus learned those things from him. However, he is inferior in this respect to (his father), in as much as a person who learns things through study is inferior to a person who knows them from practical application. The third generation must be content with imitation and, in particular, with reliance upon tradition. This member is inferior to him of the second generation, in as much as a person who relies (blindly) upon tradition is inferior to a person who exercises independent judgment.”<br /><br />“The fourth generation, then, is inferior to the preceding ones in every respect. This member has lost the qualities that preserved the edifice of their glory. He (actually) despises (those qualities). He imagines that the edifice was not built through application and effort. He thinks that it was something due his people from the very beginning by virtue of the mere fact of their (noble) descent, and not something that resulted from group (effort) and (individual) qualities.”</i><br /><br />In addition, a sedentary lifestyle <i>“leads to diversification of the desires of the belly for pleasurable food and drink. This is followed by diversification of the pleasures of sex through various ways of sexual intercourse, such as adultery and homosexuality. This leads to destruction of the (human) species.”</i><br /><br />He calls the final state of civilization <i>senility</i>, and observes that once reached it cannot be reversed. This sense of entitlement, forgetting where peace and prosperity come from, and obsessive pleasure seeking describes modern Western culture. Have we passed our four generation limit, and will we be swept away by superior <i>group feeling</i> of tribes from the desert, or by the <i>royal authority</i> of a rising China? Then again, the Roman Empire hung on for centuries after its decline began.<br /><br /><i>“God creates whatever He wishes, and His is the choice."</i><br /><br /><b>The Role of Religion</b><br /><br /><i>"Religious propaganda cannot materialize without group feeling."</i><br /><br />Many of the chapters take a very <i>terrestrial</i> view of the role of religion. Here he tells us religion will not succeed without man’s help:<br /><br /><i>“The truth one must know is that no religious or political propaganda can be successful, unless power and group feeling exist to support the religious and political aspirations and to defend them against those who reject them, until God's will with regard to them materializes. This is indicated in the afore-mentioned tradition: God sent no prophet who did not enjoy the protection of his people."</i><br /><br />And religion is not even required for a successful civilization:<br /><br /><i>“One of its premises is that the restraining influence comes into being only through a religious law from God, to which the mass submits as a matter of belief and religious creed. This premise is not acceptable. The restraining influence comes into being as the result of the impetus of royal authority and the forcefulness of the mighty, even if there is no religious law. This was the case among the Magians and other nations who had no scriptures and had not been reached by a prophetic mission.”</i><br /><br />Yet the chapters that present early Islamic history tell us that none of the major figures can do any wrong. Even when the fourth caliph ‘Ali was defeated by Mu'awiyah, and the capital was moved from spiritual Medina to sedentary Damascus, no fault with either side can be found. One is not supposed to ask if the caliphate was a victim of his four-generation rule, and the royal authority acquired by Mu'awiyah was too much to resist.<br /><br /><i>“One should beware of letting one's mind or tongue become used to criticizing any of (the ancient Muslims). One's heart should not be tempted by doubts concerning anything that happened in connection with them. They never differed among themselves except for good reasons. It should further be believed that their differences were a source of divine mercy for later Muslims, so that every (later Muslim) can take as his model the old Muslim of his choice and make him his imam, guide, and leader.”</i><br /><br />This is an interesting take on the idea that diversity is strength – you can cherry pick the tradition of your choice.<br /><br />He makes the argument that although submission to civil law weakens men by destroying their self-reliance, submission to religious law strengthens them. “When the Muslims got their religion from the Lawgiver (Muhammad), the restraining influence came from themselves.” Over time, “The influence of religion decreased among men, and they came to use restraining laws. The religious law became a branch of learning and a craft to be acquired through instruction and education.” This passage, and much else in the book, suggests that religious values were a personal choice. This conflicts with an image of Islam taken from modern Saudi Arabia or Iran. Perhaps enforcement of religious law by royal authority was not as common during his time. Or he chose not to talk about it.<br /><br /><i>“If this is understood, God's wise plans with regard to His creation and creatures will become clear.”</i><br /><br /><b>The Battle over Aristotle</b><br /><br /><i>"A refutation of philosophy. The corruption of the students of philosophy."</i><br /><br />The smoldering war between terrestrial reason and religion bursts into the open in the last few chapters. The irony here is that he is using the rational methods of Aristotle to refute reason. He makes the reasonable case that philosophy is not capable of comprehending the divine:<br /><br /><i>“The philosophers, who restrict themselves to affirming the intellect and neglect everything beyond it, are in a way comparable, to physicists who restrict themselves to affirming the body and who disregard (both) soul and intellect in the belief that there is nothing beyond the body in (God's) wise plan concerning (the world of) existence.”</i><br /><br />But the religious viewpoint is more restrictive than that:<br /><br /><i>“However, we must refrain from studying these things, since such (restraint) falls under (the duty of) the Muslim not to do what does not concern him. The problems of physics are of no importance for us in our religious affairs or our livelihoods. Therefore, we must leave them alone.”</i><br /><br />A large part of this book violates this injunction. Fortunately, he wrote it anyway, and we in later generations can benefit from his wisdom and foresight. This remarkable book is well worth reading.<br /><br /><i>"And He creates what you do not know."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9535950732720857530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_982987147" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/982987147" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30580090-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540034244p2/30580090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/982987147?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/30580090-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691120546" href="/book/show/229567.The_Muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30580090-alex?shelf=non-fic">non-fic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer982987147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer66272846360263145">This rating is very much a compromise, which is really saying something. <br/><br/>First, the positive: I cannot exaggerate my respect for Ibn Khaldun the historian, economist and sociologist. His theories on the rise and fall of medieval Islamic dynasties are so sound that the majority of modern works I've read just echo his words. He's centuries ahead of his time in terms of his attention to economic and social history, and his first chapter - debunking impossible claims by early historians from Greece,</span>
  <span id="freeText66272846360263145" style="display:none">This rating is very much a compromise, which is really saying something. <br /><br />First, the positive: I cannot exaggerate my respect for Ibn Khaldun the historian, economist and sociologist. His theories on the rise and fall of medieval Islamic dynasties are so sound that the majority of modern works I've read just echo his words. He's centuries ahead of his time in terms of his attention to economic and social history, and his first chapter - debunking impossible claims by early historians from Greece, Rome and the Caliphates with simple logic - is an amazing example of historiography. Like a lot of pre-modern historians, Ibn Khaldun's work is also...kind of charming. Unlike a lot of ancient historians, however, the charm doesn't come from inaccuracies and clever phrasing but from the meticulous put-downs of his fellow historians. It's historiography at its most catty It's also over too soon.<br /><br />The problem is, aside from a fantastic opening, actually reading the book is pretty dull. It's not helped by the difficulty of translating Arabic and the fact that even the translator's Orientalism-seeped terminology is seeming pretty dated now. (I read the 1967 Franz Rosenthal translation) Ibn Khaldun's popularity means that his views have been repeated and improved upon for hundreds of years. You don't get much out of reading his exact words that you won't get out of a decent modern historian writing in English...and you might have to sit through a lot of discredited and not particularly interesting medical knowledge to get there.<br /><br />The Muqaddimah has a certain charm, and its author is all kinds of amazing, but it's too old to be of use to people looking for theories of history and too difficult to translate for people looking for fascinating stories. Look for a modern historical work about Ibn Khaldun or, even better, a historian who cites him as an inspiration. The genius of Ibn Khaldun's work is that you can understand his ideas without reading his exact words.</span>
  <a data-text-id="66272846360263145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_98090970" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98090970" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1662632-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540855273p2/1662632.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98090970?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/1662632-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691017549" href="/book/show/1427579.The_Muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Richard by: </span>
      Melvyn Bragg
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=social-political">social-political</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98090970" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1371060584768011301">I decided to read this after hearing the <i>In Our Time</i> podcast by Melvyn Bragg about this author (listen <a href='http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b00qckbw' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>). Blurb:<blockquote>Melvyn Bragg and guests Robert Hoyland, Robert Irwin, and Hugh Kennedy discuss the life and ideas of the 14th-century Arab philosopher of history Ibn Khaldun.<br/><br/>Ibn Khaldun was a North African statesman who retreated into the desert in 1375. He emerged having written one of the most important ever studies of the workings of history.<br/><br/>Khaldun was born in Tunis in 1332. He received a supr</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1371060584768011301" style="display:none">I decided to read this after hearing the <i>In Our Time</i> podcast by Melvyn Bragg about this author (listen <a href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b00qckbw" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>). Blurb:<blockquote>Melvyn Bragg and guests Robert Hoyland, Robert Irwin, and Hugh Kennedy discuss the life and ideas of the 14th-century Arab philosopher of history Ibn Khaldun.<br /><br />Ibn Khaldun was a North African statesman who retreated into the desert in 1375. He emerged having written one of the most important ever studies of the workings of history.<br /><br />Khaldun was born in Tunis in 1332. He received a supremely good education, but at 16 lost many of his family to the Black Death. His adult life was similarly characterized by sharp turns of fortune. He built a career as a political operator in cities from Fez to Granada. But he often fared badly in court intrigues, was imprisoned and failed to prevent the murder of a fellow statesman.<br /><br />In 1375, he withdrew into the Sahara to work out why the Muslim world had degenerated into division and decline. Four years later, he had completed not only a history of North African politics but also, in the book's long introduction, one of the great studies of history.<br /><br />Drawing on both regional history and personal experience, he set out a bleak analysis of the rise and fall of dynasties. He argued that group solidarity was vital to success in power. Within five generations, though, this always decayed. Tired urban dynasties inevitably became vulnerable to overthrow by rural insurgents.<br /><br />Later in life, Ibn Khaldun worked as a judge in Egypt, and in 1401 he met the terrifying Mongol conqueror Tamburlaine, whose triumphs, Ibn Khaldun felt, bore out his pessimistic theories.<br /><br />Over the last three centuries, Ibn Khaldun has been rediscovered as a profoundly prescient political scientist, philosopher of history and forerunner of sociology -- one of the great thinkers of the Muslim world.<br /><br /><i>Robert Hoyland is Professor of Islamic History at the University of Oxford; Robert Irwin is Senior Research Associate of the School of Oriental and African Studies at the University of London; Hugh Kennedy is Professor of Arabic in the School of Oriental and African Studies at the University of London.</i></blockquote>Unfortunately, the <i>idea</i> of this was more interesting than reading it. This is essentially an instruction manual for operating a government and understanding civilization, and <i>given when and where it was written</i>, it shows an astonishing synthesis of a great deal of complex information ... but the actual content itself is somewhat banal.<br /><br />I really recommend listening to the podcast to learn a bit about history that most of us are completely ignorant of. But the book — not so much. Once I got the gist of it, I was pretty much skimming.<br />­</span>
  <a data-text-id="1371060584768011301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_327647859" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/327647859" />
    <a title="Will" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2263507-will"><img alt="Will" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250043808p2/2263507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/327647859?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will" href="/user/show/2263507-will">Will</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691120546" href="/book/show/229567.The_Muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer327647859" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10220404131455319964">This is one of those books that I never would have known to read, if not for the guidance of a good instructor. The guy who assigned it was a really smart cooky, and very helpful to boot. I hope he has gone on to an illustrious career in the history field. <br/><br/>Khaldun's writing remains one of the smartest things I've ever read. His theory bears a sophistication that the West couldn't manage until the mid-19th century. And I think the fact he was forgotten actually bears out his theory on the cyclica</span>
  <span id="freeText10220404131455319964" style="display:none">This is one of those books that I never would have known to read, if not for the guidance of a good instructor. The guy who assigned it was a really smart cooky, and very helpful to boot. I hope he has gone on to an illustrious career in the history field. <br /><br />Khaldun's writing remains one of the smartest things I've ever read. His theory bears a sophistication that the West couldn't manage until the mid-19th century. And I think the fact he was forgotten actually bears out his theory on the cyclicality of events. <br /><br />I also think that you can see Khaldun's "three generations" theory borne out in world events. His story goes like this (if I recall): Dynasties seldom last more than three generations. The first king, who fights and acquires power, knows the real value of his acquisition. So he does nothing that his potential rivals won't tolerate. His administration is thus relatively just and wise. His son knows of his struggle only by his father's word, and knows the art of statecraft from his father's example. He will govern OK, but cracks will start to show, and the seeds of dissatisfaction will be sown. The third-generation king knows only a life of luxury, and his grandfather's struggle is dimly remembered at best. He mistakenly thinks that the right to rule inheres in his person, rather than in the consent of those who tolerate his power. He is thus likely to overstep the acceptable bounds, to abuse his power, and to be displaced as a result. Now the dynasty has ended.<br /><br />I think you see this in many historical triads, and not just those that have explicit monarchies. If it's correct, it would predict that Kim Jong-un will be the last of the Kims to rule North Korea, but the Al-Assad dynasty in Syria may have one more generation before it collapses. <br /><br />In short, I recommend that people who teach intellectual history and theory courses add this to their assigned readings. Some of their students will be grateful they did. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10220404131455319964" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1113543237" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1113543237" />
    <a title="Search" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4537004-search"><img alt="Search" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1477511478p2/4537004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1113543237?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Search" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Search" href="/user/show/4537004-search">Search</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691166285" href="/book/show/23528925-the-muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4537004-search?shelf=mysticism">mysticism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4537004-search?shelf=2014">2014</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1113543237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1152099633137349276">My purpose in reading this book was very specific, this book is pretty large and looks in a huge number of subjects. I was only interested in the chapters on the supernatural. The Muqaddamah provided an excellent look into the workings of the occult, eye popping really. Ibn Khuldun gives eye witness accounts of many forms of sorcery and magic being practiced in his times as well as a little explanation of the science behind these fell arts. <br/>Well worth the read. Alot of enlightening material.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1315478573" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1315478573" />
    <a title="Barnaby Thieme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1944947-barnaby-thieme"><img alt="Barnaby Thieme" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255549111p2/1944947.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1315478573?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barnaby Thieme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barnaby Thieme" href="/user/show/1944947-barnaby-thieme">Barnaby Thieme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691120546" href="/book/show/229567.The_Muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1944947-barnaby-thieme?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1944947-barnaby-thieme?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1944947-barnaby-thieme?shelf=islam-sufism">islam-sufism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1315478573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13701303345850797156">Ibn Khaldoun's Muqaddimah is frequently described as a work of proto-sociology and economics. There is a grain of truth to that, but the similarity lies more in the subject matter than the manner of inquiry. <br/><br/>I think the scientific virtues of this book have been somewhat exaggerated, in part because of its enthusiastic reception by systematic historians such as Toynbee who were making their own effort to create or discover a general theory of history. But to my eyes, Ibn Khaldoun's method is more</span>
  <span id="freeText13701303345850797156" style="display:none">Ibn Khaldoun's Muqaddimah is frequently described as a work of proto-sociology and economics. There is a grain of truth to that, but the similarity lies more in the subject matter than the manner of inquiry. <br /><br />I think the scientific virtues of this book have been somewhat exaggerated, in part because of its enthusiastic reception by systematic historians such as Toynbee who were making their own effort to create or discover a general theory of history. But to my eyes, Ibn Khaldoun's method is more that of a speculative philosopher than a scientist. He infers general patterns on the basis of a small number of examples, and regards the patterns as prior to the actuality. <br /><br />The scientific approach would be somewhat the other way around, where the empirical example would provoke a hypothesis that would then be tested on further examples. But Ibn Khaldoun moves very quickly to a state of epistemic closure, precisely of the kind I find endemic to the Islamic thought-world of his era, and beyond. <br /><br />Rather than reading this book as a progressive predecessor to the scientific revolution, I position it as a conservative work that attempts to maintain something of the rational-empirical method of the High Middle Ages in the face of its waning under the burgeoning influence of al-Ghazali. I see this book not as the forecast of the sciences of sociology and economics, but as a late example of the rationalism that had been typical of much of the thought of al-Andalus and the 'Abbasid caliphate. <br /><br />I think few of his actual statements of fact will be too persuasive for the modern reader, from his position that the sun is neither hot nor cold to his view that blacks are well known to be less intelligent to his view that royalty proceeds from holy authority, and urban settled life proceeds from both. But this is a work of some interest to the intellectual historian. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13701303345850797156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1883535878" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1883535878" />
    <a title="Joshua Powell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21826061-joshua-powell"><img alt="Joshua Powell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483930330p2/21826061.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1883535878?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshua Powell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshua Powell" href="/user/show/21826061-joshua-powell">Joshua Powell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780691017549" href="/book/show/1427579.The_Muqaddimah">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1883535878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14290830912650476007">"Undoubtedly the greatest work of its kind that has ever been created by any mind in any time or place... the most comprehensive and illuminating analysis of how human affairs work that has been made anywhere." - Arnold J Toynbee, observer<br/><br/>I'm inclined to agree with Mr Toynbee here, but of course - has anyone ever tried to create a work that even closely parallels this? This book is sheer madness and he says he wrote it in five months. If a 20th century update had existed it certainly would have </span>
  <span id="freeText14290830912650476007" style="display:none">"Undoubtedly the greatest work of its kind that has ever been created by any mind in any time or place... the most comprehensive and illuminating analysis of how human affairs work that has been made anywhere." - Arnold J Toynbee, observer<br /><br />I'm inclined to agree with Mr Toynbee here, but of course - has anyone ever tried to create a work that even closely parallels this? This book is sheer madness and he says he wrote it in five months. If a 20th century update had existed it certainly would have been included in the inventory of Voyager.<br /><br />This book is almost pre-apocalyptic, I imagine Ibn Khaldun in desperation trying to preserve every-single-thing-ever about humanity before its collapse. This is post-crusade and mid-plague and in the twilight of the Islamic empire, so if Khaldun really was desperate to consolidate and preserve he wouldn't have been too far off. Of course, he does intend for someone in the future to use this as a jumping-off point for a second edition, so what I just said is easily and immediately refutable, but I still can't help but wonder whether that played any motivating role at all. What I can't imagine however is Khaldun - or anyone at all for that matter - being able to or even bother trying to write something comparable today. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14290830912650476007" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18153546889465217860">Criminally ignored in history of philosophy/intellectual history, since it shockingly illustrates the different ways European & Arabic philosophy were working. This work, written in the 1300s, predates & anticipates a fantastic number of "unique" and "milestone" breakthroughs in European philosophy, including:<br/><br/>Limits of induction (Hume), compromise between rationalism & empiricism (Kant), inaccessibility of the noumenal world (also Kant), Labor theory of value (Smith/Marx), necessity of interpret</span>
  <span id="freeText18153546889465217860" style="display:none">Criminally ignored in history of philosophy/intellectual history, since it shockingly illustrates the different ways European &amp; Arabic philosophy were working. This work, written in the 1300s, predates &amp; anticipates a fantastic number of "unique" and "milestone" breakthroughs in European philosophy, including:<br /><br />Limits of induction (Hume), compromise between rationalism &amp; empiricism (Kant), inaccessibility of the noumenal world (also Kant), Labor theory of value (Smith/Marx), necessity of interpretation due to cultural/linguistic relativity (Vico/Herder), Truth as intellectual consensus conforming to empirical observation (Popper), tension between truth in text and truth in speech (Derrida), language influences thought (Whorf), the power necessarily inherent in law (Weber/Foucault)<br /><br />and probably some others as well</span>

  <span id="freeText251427611551521882" style="display:none">I can't say anything about it, would just copy the statement of Franz Rosenthal on the Muqaddimah:<br /><br />It can be regarded as the earliest attempt made by any historian to discover a pattern in the changes that occur in man's political and social organization.         rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65783303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5837278048939613505">A fascinating look at the history (and geography) of the world from the perspective of a well-traveled Islamic scholar/politician in the 14th century (late golden age of Islam). It also includes, among many other things, a detailed discussion of how to tell real prophets from fake. I remember that part, I think, because it drove home for me how very different his world and mindset are from mine.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2111684310" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2111684310" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1098316-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2111684310?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/1098316-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9789054601586" href="/book/show/18335326-de-muqaddima">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2111684310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3981595212954578008">Interesting account of Islamic history and the evolution of societies. I particularly liked the Epistle instructions that the secretary 'Abd-al-Hamid' addressed to his fellow secretaries:<br/><br/>And now: May God guard you who practice the craft of<br/>secretaryship, and may He keep you and give you success and<br/>guidance. There are prophets and messengers and highly honored<br/>kings. After them come different kinds of men, all of them made by<br/>God. They are of different kinds, even if they are all alike in fact.<br/>God o</span>
  <span id="freeText3981595212954578008" style="display:none">Interesting account of Islamic history and the evolution of societies. I particularly liked the Epistle instructions that the secretary 'Abd-al-Hamid' addressed to his fellow secretaries:<br /><br />And now: May God guard you who practice the craft of<br />secretaryship, and may He keep you and give you success and<br />guidance. There are prophets and messengers and highly honored<br />kings. After them come different kinds of men, all of them made by<br />God. They are of different kinds, even if they are all alike in fact.<br />God occupied them with different kinds of crafts and various sorts of<br />businesses, so that they might be able to make a living and earn their<br />sustenance. He gave to you, assembled secretaries, the great<br />opportunity to be men of education and gentlemen, to have<br />knowledge and (good) judgment.505 You bring out whatever is good<br />in the caliphate and straighten out its affairs. Through your advice,<br />God improves the government for the benefit of human beings and<br />makes their countries civilized. The ruler cannot dispense with you.<br />You alone make him a competent ruler. Your position with regard to<br />rulers is that (you are) the ears through which they hear, the eyes<br />through which they see, the tongues through which they speak, and<br />the hands through which they touch. May God give you, therefore,<br />enjoyment of the excellent craft with which He has distinguished you,<br />and may He not deprive you of the great favors that He has shown<br />unto you.<br />No craftsman needs more than you to combine all praiseworthy good traits<br />and all memorable and highly regarded excellent qualities, O secretaries, if you<br />aspire to fit the description given of you in this letter. The secretary needs on his<br />own account, and his master, who trusts him with his important affairs, expects him,<br />to be mild where mildness is needed, to be understanding where judgment is<br />needed, to be enterprising where enterprise is needed, to be hesitant where<br />hesitation is needed. He must prefer modesty, justice, and fairness. He must keep<br />secrets. He must be faithful in difficult circumstances. He must know (beforehand)<br />about the calamities that may come. He must be able to put things in their proper<br />places and misfortunes into their proper categories. He must have studied every<br />branch of learning and know it well, and if he does not know it well, he must at<br />least have acquired an adequate amount of it. By virtue of his natural intelligence,<br />good education, and outstanding experience, he must know what is going to happen<br />to him before it happens, and he must know the result of his actions before action<br />starts. He must make the proper preparations for everything, and he must set up<br />everything in its proper, customary form.<br />Therefore, assembled secretaries, vie with each other to acquire the different<br />kinds of education and to gain an understanding of religious matters. Start with<br />knowledge of the Book of God and religious duties. Then, study the Arabic<br />language, as that will give you a cultivated form of speech. Then, learn to write well,<br />as that will be an ornament to your letters. Transmit poetry and acquaint yourselves<br />with the rare expressions and ideas that poems contain. Acquaint yourselves also<br />with both Arab and nonArab political events, and with the tales of (both groups) and<br />the biographies describing them, as that will be helpful to you in your endeavors. Do<br />not neglect to study accounting, for it is the mainstay of the land tax register. 506<br />Detest prejudices with all your heart, lofty ones as well as low ones, and all idle and<br />contemptible things, for they bring humility and are the ruin of secretaryship. Do not<br />let your craft be a low one. Guard against backbiting and calumny and the actions of<br />stupid people. Beware of haughtiness, foolishness, and pride, for they mean<br />acquiring hostility without (even the excuse of) hatred. Love each other in God in<br />your craft. Advise your colleagues to practice it in a way befitting your virtuous,<br />fair, and gifted predecessors.<br />If times go hard for one of you, be kind to him and console him, until<br />everything be well with him again. Should old age make one of you unable to get<br />around and pursue his livelihood and meet his friends, visit him and honor him and<br />consult him, and profit from his outstanding experience and mature knowledge.<br />Every one of you should be more concerned for his assistants, who may be useful<br />when needed, than for his own children or brothers. Should some praise come (to<br />one of you) in the course of his work, he should ascribe the merit to his colleague;<br />any blame he should bear all by himself. He should beware of mistakes and slips<br />and of being annoyed when conditions change. For you, assembled secretaries, are<br />more prompt to be blamed than Qur'an readers,507 and blame is more detrimental to<br />you than to them. You know that everyone of you has a master, one who gives from<br />his own as much as can be expected, and (every one of you) has the obligation to<br />repay him, since he deserves it, with fidelity, gratefulness, tolerance, patience, good<br />counsel, discretion, and active interest in his affairs, and to show (his good<br />intentions) by his actions whenever his master needs him and his resources. Be<br />conscious of (your obligations) - God give you success - in good and bad<br />circumstances, in privation as in munificence and kindness, in happiness as in<br />misfortune. Any member of this noble craft who has all these qualities has good<br />qualities indeed.<br />If any one of you be appointed to an office, or if some matter that concerns<br />God's children be turned over to one of you, he should think of God and choose<br />obedience to Him. He should be kind to the weak and fair to those who have been<br />wronged. All creatures are God's children. He loves most those who are kindest to<br />His children. Furthermore, he should judge with justice, he should honor the noble<br />(descendants of Muhammad), augment the booty (gained in wars against infidels),<br />and bring civilization to the country. He should be friendly to the subjects, and<br />refrain from harming them. He should be humble and mild in his office. He should<br />be kind in handling the land tax registers 508 and in calling in outstanding claims.<br />You should explore the character of him with whom you associate. When his<br />good and bad sides are known, you will be able to help him to do the good things<br />that agree with him, and be able to contrive to keep him from the bad things he<br />desires. You must be able to do that in the subtlest and best manner. You know that<br />a person who is in charge of an animal and understands his job, endeavors to know<br />the character of the animal. If it is inclined to gallop,509 he does not goad it when he<br />is riding it. If it is inclined to kick, he takes precautions with its forelegs. If he fears<br />that it will shy, he takes precautions with its head. If it is restive, he gently subdues<br />its desire to go where it wants to go. If it still continues, he pulls it slightly to the<br />side, then has its halter loosened. This description of how to take care of an animal<br />contains good points for those who want to lead human beings and deal with them,<br />serve them, and have intimate contact with them. The secretary, with his excellent<br />education, his noble craft, his subtlety, his frequent dealings with people who confer<br />with him and discuss things with him and learn from him or fear his severity, needs<br />to be kind to his associates,509a to flatter them, and to supply their wants, even more<br />than the person in charge of an animal which cannot answer, does not know what is<br />right, does not understand what is said to it, and goes only where its master who<br />rides upon it makes it go. Be kind - God show mercy unto you-when you look after<br />things. Use as much reflection and thought as possible. God permitting, you will<br />thus escape harshness, annoyance, and rudeness on the part of your associates. They<br />will be in agreement with you, and you will have their friendship and protection, if<br />God wills.<br />None of you should have too sumptuous an office or go beyond the proper<br />limits in his dress, his mount, his food, his drink, his house, his servants, or in the<br />other things pertaining to his station, for, despite the nobility of the craft by which<br />God has distinguished you, you are servants who are not permitted to fall short in<br />their service. You are caretakers whom one does not permit to be wasteful or<br />spendthrift. Try to preserve your modesty by planned moderation in all the things I<br />have mentioned and told you. Beware of the wastefulness of prodigality and the bad<br />results of luxury. They engender poverty and bring about humiliation. People who<br />(are prodigal and live in luxury) are put to shame, especially if they be secretaries<br />and men of education.<br />Things repeat themselves. One thing contains the clue to another. Let<br />yourselves be guided in your future undertakings-by your previous experience.<br />Then, choose the method of doing things that is most definite, most accurate, and<br />that promises the best result. You should know that there is something that defeats<br />accomplishment, namely, talking about things. The person who does it is prevented<br />from using his knowledge and his ability to think. Therefore, everyone of you, while<br />he is in his office, should endeavor to talk no more than is sufficient; he should be<br />concise in the matters he brings up and in the answers he gives; and he should give<br />thought to all the arguments he advances. His work will profit from that. It will<br />prevent too much preoccupation with other things. He should implore God to grant<br />him success and to support him with His guidance, for he must fear making mistakes<br />that might hurt his body and (cast doubt upon) his intelligence and education. When<br />any one of you says or thinks that the high quality and efficiency of his work is<br />obviously the result of his own cleverness and knowledge of how to do things, he<br />provokes God. God will let him depend upon himself alone, and then he will find<br />that he is not adequate to his task. This is no secret to those who reflect.<br />None of you should say that he has a better understanding of affairs, or<br />knows better how to handle difficult matters, than other members of his craft, than<br />those who serve together with him. Of two persons, discerning people consider him<br />the more intelligent who throws off conceit and thinks his colleagues more<br />intelligent and more skillful than he. But at any rate, both parties should<br />acknowledge the excellence of God's favors. No one should let himself be deceived<br />by his own opinions and consider himself free from mistakes. Nor should he strive<br />to outdo his friends, equals, colleagues, or his family. Everybody must give praise to<br />God, in humility in the face of His greatness, in meekness in the face of His might,<br />and in fulfillment of the command to speak of God's favors.510<br />In this letter of mine, let me refer to the old proverb: "He who accepts good<br />advice 511 all is successful." This is the essence of this letter and the best that is said<br />in it, after the references to God it contains. Therefore, I have placed it at the end,<br />and I close the letter with it. May God take care of us and of you, assembled<br />students and secretaries, in the same way He takes care of those whom, as He knows<br />in His prescience, He will make happy and guide aright. He can do it. It is in His<br />hand.<br />Farewell, and God's mercy and blessings upon you </span>
  <a data-text-id="3981595212954578008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2296795884" class="readable"
